			</item>
		<item>
		<title>Enhancing enterprise inference on Amazon SageMaker HyperPod with data capture, Hugging Face, NVMe, and Route 53 integration</title>
		<link>https://aws.amazon.com/blogs/machine-learning/enhancing-enterprise-inference-on-amazon-sagemaker-hyperpod-with-data-capture-hugging-face-nvme-and-route-53-integration/</link>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Vinay Arora]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Thu, 09 Jul 2026 16:38:11 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Advanced (300)]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Amazon Elastic Kubernetes Service]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Amazon SageMaker HyperPod]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Technical How-to]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">2d41e2165c4d62f27faee9937bc229c44251b5a6</guid>

					<description>In this post, we walk through five capabilities now available in SageMaker HyperPod inference: multi-tier data capture for auditing and model improvement, direct deployment from Hugging Face Hub, local NVMe model loading for faster cold starts, automated Route 53 DNS for custom domains, and pod-level IAM through custom service accounts.</description>
										<content:encoded>&lt;p&gt;As enterprises scale their generative AI workloads, the demand for faster, more observable, and more flexible inference infrastructure continues to grow. Amazon SageMaker HyperPod is rising to meet that challenge with a set of new capabilities designed to streamline how organizations deploy and operate large models in production. Teams can now record inputs and outputs at multiple points along the inference path: from the endpoint, to the load balancer, to the model pod itself. This provides deep observability and auditability through declarative custom resource definition (CRD) configuration. You can also deploy models directly from popular community hubs without the need to pre-stage weights in object or file storage, with built-in support for gated access, revision pinning, and token isolation across leading inference runtimes such as vLLM, TGI, and SGLang.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Beyond deployment, these enhancements deliver meaningful performance and security gains. Loading weights from node-local NVMe storage reduces cold-start latency, with automatic fallback to cloud storage when needed. HyperPod automatically manages custom domain DNS records for you, while granular pod-level AWS Identity and Access Management (IAM) permissions give infrastructure teams fine-grained control over security boundaries. Together, these capabilities create a more performant, secure, and enterprise-ready inference experience on HyperPod. Teams can ship AI applications faster without compromising on governance or operational visibility.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/07/08/ML-21090-1-1.png" alt="Inference architecture on Amazon SageMaker HyperPod showing request flow from the endpoint to the load balancer to the model pod" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;Inference on Amazon SageMaker HyperPod&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="inference-data-capture"&gt;Inference data capture&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;With Amazon SageMaker HyperPod inference data capture, you can record inference request and response data for model monitoring, debugging, and model improvement. Inference requests flow from the Amazon SageMaker AI endpoint to the Application Load Balancer and then to the model pod. You independently control and configure data capture at each level, giving you flexibility to choose the right depth of visibility for your use case.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="prerequisite"&gt;Prerequisite&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The following are prerequisites before you enable the data capture feature.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;You need an Amazon Simple Storage Service (Amazon S3) bucket (using a URI like s3://amzn-s3-demo-bucket or s3://amzn-s3-demo-bucket/prefix) and the right IAM permissions for the operator to write to it. If you don’t provide one, the system uses the TLS certificate bucket instead.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Each tier has its own required settings. For the SageMaker Endpoint level, turn it on, set a destination S3 URI, pick at least one capture option (input, output, or both), and choose a sampling percentage. For the Load Balancer level, enable it with an s3:// URI. For the Model Pod level, enable it. It defaults to capturing both input and output at 100 percent sampling.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;While not required, we recommend that you add an AWS Key Management Service (AWS KMS) key so you can encrypt your captured data. You can also fine-tune the buffer settings (batch size and flush interval) and set up content-type headers for CSV/JSON handling along with payload size limits.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="capture-tiers"&gt;Capture tiers&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Data capture supports three tiers, each capturing at a different point in the request flow. You can enable any combination:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Tier 1 – SageMaker AI endpoint&lt;/strong&gt; ({s3Uri}/{hash}/sme/): Captures full input and output payloads at the SageMaker AI Runtime API boundary. This tier requires you to register the endpoint. Use Tier 1 when you need compatibility with SageMaker AI Model Monitor.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Tier 2 – Application Load Balancer&lt;/strong&gt; ({s3Uri}/{hash}/alb/): Turns on ALB access logs, which capture request metadata such as client IPs, request paths, and latencies.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Tier 3 – Model pod&lt;/strong&gt; ({s3Uri}/{hash}/pod/): Captures full inference input and output payloads at the inference container with configurable sampling, buffering, and payload size limits. Works without SageMaker AI endpoint registration. Use Tier 3 when you need the deepest visibility closest to the model.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="configuring-data-capture"&gt;Configuring data capture&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Enable data capture by adding a dataCapture section to your InferenceEndpointConfig or JumpStartModel CRD. The following example shows the overall structure with all three tiers enabled:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-yaml"&gt;dataCapture:
  s3Uri: s3://amzn-s3-demo-bucket/captures/  # Optional. Defaults to TLS bucket.
  sagemakerEndpoint:
    enabled: true
    initialSamplingPercentage: 100
    kmsKeyId: arn:aws:kms:us-east-2:123456789012:key/my-key-id
    captureOptions:
      - captureMode: Input
      - captureMode: Output
    captureContentTypeHeader:
      jsonContentTypes:
        - application/json
  loadBalancer:
    enabled: true
  modelPod:
    enabled: true
    initialSamplingPercentage: 100
    kmsKeyId: arn:aws:kms:us-east-2:123456789012:key/my-key-id
    captureOptions:
      - captureMode: Input
      - captureMode: Output
    bufferConfig:
      batchSize: 100
      flushIntervalSeconds: 60
    payloadConfig:
      maxPayloadSizeKB: 1024&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="s3-storage-behavior"&gt;S3 storage behavior&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;All tiers write to your Amazon S3 bucket. If you don’t specify an s3Uri, HyperPod stores your data in the TLS certificate bucket under a /data-capture/ prefix by default. Within the bucket, each deployment gets a unique path based on a hash derived from the cluster ARN, namespace, CRD type, and deployment name. The same deployment always generates the same prefix, so data capture artifacts from multiple CRD submissions targeting the same deployment flow to the same S3 subfolder.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="iam-permissions"&gt;IAM permissions&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To enable data capture on existing clusters, add the following S3 permission to your Inference Operator Execution Role:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-json"&gt;{
  "Sid": "DataCaptureS3Access",
  "Effect": "Allow",
  "Action": "s3:PutObject",
  "Resource": "arn:aws:s3:::hyperpod-tls*/data-capture/*",
  "Condition": {
    "StringEquals": {
      "aws:ResourceAccount": "${aws:PrincipalAccount}"
    }
  }
}&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;If you use a customer-managed KMS key, also add:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-json"&gt;{
  "Sid": "DataCaptureKmsAccess",
  "Effect": "Allow",
  "Action": [
    "kms:Decrypt",
    "kms:GenerateDataKey"
  ],
  "Resource": "arn:aws:kms:*:*:key/*",
  "Condition": {
    "StringLike": {
      "kms:ViaService": "s3.*.amazonaws.com",
      "kms:EncryptionContext:aws:s3:arn": "arn:aws:s3:::hyperpod-tls*"
    },
    "StringEquals": {
      "aws:ResourceAccount": "${aws:PrincipalAccount}"
    }
  }
}&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="best-practices"&gt;Best practices&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Follow these recommendations to optimize data capture for cost, security, and operational efficiency.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Use initialSamplingPercentage to control the volume of captured data. Start with a lower percentage in production and increase as needed.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Use payloadConfig.maxPayloadSizeKB (Tier 3) to cap the size of captured payloads and control storage costs.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Specify a kmsKeyId for Tier 1 and Tier 3 if your workload requires encryption at rest with your own KMS key.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;ALB access logs (Tier 2) capture request metadata including URLs and query parameters. Use POST request bodies rather than query parameters for sensitive inputs.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To disable data capture for a tier, set its enabled field to false or remove the tier section from your CRD. To disable all data capture, remove the dataCapture section entirely.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For more information, see &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/latest/dg/sagemaker-hyperpod-model-deployment-data-capture.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Data capture for inference on HyperPod&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="hugging-face-model-source"&gt;Hugging Face model source&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Deploy models directly from Hugging Face Hub without pre-staging to Amazon S3 or Amazon FSx. This source supports gated models through tokenSecretRef, revision pinning through commitSHA, and token isolation. It is compatible with vLLM, TGI, and SGLang runtimes. See &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/latest/dg/sagemaker-hyperpod-model-deployment-deploy-ftm.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Deploy models from Amazon S3, Amazon FSx, or Hugging Face Hub using kubectl&lt;/a&gt; for details. For the Hugging Face source, the Inference Operator emits Kubernetes events for any failures or success states to track the status of the deployment.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="prerequisites"&gt;Prerequisites&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To deploy Hugging Face models, you must provide the following prerequisite fields based on the model chosen.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Provide a valid &lt;code&gt;modelId&lt;/code&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;A Kubernetes Secret with your Hugging Face token (for gated models).&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;A GPU-enabled worker with a volume mount for the weights you download.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="steps-to-deploy-a-hugging-face-model"&gt;Steps to deploy a Hugging Face model&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;ol type="1"&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Create a Kubernetes Secret containing your Hugging Face API token. You need this token for gated models and should use it for all downloads. You can generate a token at &lt;a href="https://huggingface.co/settings/tokens" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;huggingface.co/settings/tokens&lt;/a&gt;. 
  &lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
   &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;kubectl create secret generic hf-token-secret --from-literal=token=hf_YOUR_TOKEN_HERE \
  -n $CLUSTER_NAMESPACE&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
  &lt;/div&gt; &lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Set up a SageMaker endpoint name. 
  &lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
   &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;export SAGEMAKER_ENDPOINT_NAME="mistral7b-hf"&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
  &lt;/div&gt; &lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Construct the following sample YAML. For the entire deployment YAML, see the Hugging Face section in &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/latest/dg/sagemaker-hyperpod-model-deployment-deploy-ftm.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Deploy models from Amazon S3, Amazon FSx, or Hugging Face Hub using kubectl&lt;/a&gt; 
  &lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
   &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-yaml"&gt;modelName: mistral-7b
modelSourceConfig:
  modelSourceType: huggingface
  prefetchEnabled: true
  huggingFaceModel:
    modelId: "mistralai/Mistral-7B-Instruct-v0.3"
    tokenSecretRef:
      name: hf-token-secret
      key: token
instanceType: "ml.g5.24xlarge"&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
  &lt;/div&gt; &lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Deploy the YAML file with the kubectl command: 
  &lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
   &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;kubectl apply -f deploy_hf_inference.yaml&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
  &lt;/div&gt; &lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Check if the model successfully deployed and endpoint created successfully. 
  &lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
   &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;kubectl describe InferenceEndpointConfig $SAGEMAKER_ENDPOINT_NAME -n $CLUSTER_NAMESPACE
kubectl describe SageMakerEndpointRegistration $SAGEMAKER_ENDPOINT_NAME -n $CLUSTER_NAMESPACE&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
  &lt;/div&gt; &lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;To check the status of the deployment and debug errors check the Kubernetes events 
  &lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
   &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;kubectl get events -n $CLUSTER_NAMESPACE&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
  &lt;/div&gt; &lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Test the deployed endpoint to verify it’s working correctly. This step confirms that your model is successfully deployed and can process inference requests. 
  &lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
   &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;aws sagemaker-runtime invoke-endpoint \
  --endpoint-name $SAGEMAKER_ENDPOINT_NAME \
  --content-type "application/json" \
  --body '{"inputs": "What is AWS SageMaker?"}' \
  --region $REGION \
  --cli-binary-format raw-in-base64-out \
  /dev/stdout&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
  &lt;/div&gt; &lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ol&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="route-53-dns-management"&gt;Route 53 DNS management&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;You can automatically create and manage DNS records for custom domains through dnsConfig. For more information, see Custom certificates and Route 53 DNS management for HyperPod Inference.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Amazon SageMaker HyperPod inference now integrates with Amazon Route 53 to automatically create and manage DNS records for your inference endpoints. Specify a hosted zone ID in your CRD and the operator handles record creation, updates, and cleanup for your custom domain.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="prerequisites-1"&gt;Prerequisites&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Before configuring Route 53 DNS management, make sure you have the following:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;An AWS Certificate Manager (ACM) certificate in the Issued state covering your domain.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;A Route 53 hosted zone for your domain.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="iam-permissions-1"&gt;IAM permissions&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Add the following permissions to your Inference Operator execution role:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-json"&gt;{
  "Version": "2012-10-17",
  "Statement": [
    {
      "Sid": "ACMCustomCertificateAccess",
      "Effect": "Allow",
      "Action": [
        "acm:DescribeCertificate",
        "acm:GetCertificate"
      ],
      "Resource": "arn:aws:acm:&amp;lt;region&amp;gt;:&amp;lt;account-id&amp;gt;:certificate/*"
    },
    {
      "Sid": "S3CertificateUpload",
      "Effect": "Allow",
      "Action": [
        "s3:PutObject",
        "s3:PutObjectTagging"
      ],
      "Resource": "arn:aws:s3:::&amp;lt;tls-certificate-bucket&amp;gt;/*",
      "Condition": {
        "StringEquals": {
          "s3:RequestObjectTag/CreatedBy": "HyperPodInference"
        }
      }
    },
    {
      "Sid": "Route53DNSManagement",
      "Effect": "Allow",
      "Action": [
        "route53:GetHostedZone",
        "route53:ListResourceRecordSets",
        "route53:ChangeResourceRecordSets"
      ],
      "Resource": "arn:aws:route53:::hostedzone/&amp;lt;hosted-zone-id&amp;gt;"
    }
  ]
}&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Replace &lt;code&gt;&amp;lt;region&amp;gt;&lt;/code&gt;, &lt;code&gt;&amp;lt;account-id&amp;gt;&lt;/code&gt;, &lt;code&gt;&amp;lt;tls-certificate-bucket&amp;gt;&lt;/code&gt;, and &lt;code&gt;&amp;lt;hosted-zone-id&amp;gt;&lt;/code&gt; with your actual values. If your S3 bucket name starts with &lt;code&gt;hyperpod-tls&lt;/code&gt;, the S3 permissions are already included in the &lt;code&gt;AmazonSageMakerHyperPodInferenceAccess&lt;/code&gt; managed policy and you only need the ACM and Route 53 statements.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="configuring-dns-management"&gt;Configuring DNS management&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Enable Route 53 DNS management by adding tlsConfig and dnsConfig to your model deployment YAML:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-yaml"&gt;tlsConfig:
  customCertificateConfig:
    acmArn: arn:aws:acm:us-west-2:123456789012:certificate/abc12345-1234-1234-1234-abc123456789
    domainName: api.example.com
    tlsCertificateOutputS3Uri: s3://my-tls-bucket
dnsConfig:
  hostedZoneId: Z1234567890ABC&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The domainName must match a domain in your ACM certificate. For wildcard certificates (for example, &lt;code&gt;*.example.com&lt;/code&gt;), specify the specific subdomain (for example, &lt;code&gt;api.example.com&lt;/code&gt;).&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="verify-dns-status"&gt;Verify DNS status&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;kubectl describe InferenceEndpointConfig my-model -n my-namespace&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Check the dnsStatus section:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-plaintext"&gt;Status:
  Dns Status:
    Managed By Operator: true
    Record Name: api.example.com
    Hosted Zone Id: Z1234567890ABC
    Dns Health: Active
    Message: DNS record resolves successfully&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;After DNS health shows Active, you can access your endpoint at your custom domain.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For more information about custom domains and TLS configuration, see &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/latest/dg/sagemaker-hyperpod-model-deployment-custom-certs.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Custom certificates and Route 53 DNS management for HyperPod Inference&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="local-nvme-model-deployment"&gt;Local NVMe model deployment&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Amazon SageMaker HyperPod supports loading model weights directly from a node’s local NVMe storage instead of pulling them over the network from Amazon S3 or Amazon FSx. When you read weights locally, you remove the network hop during pod startup, which helps you reduce inference pod cold-start time. You can use this approach for autoscaling events, scale-from-zero workloads, and latency-sensitive failovers. Local NVMe instance storage is typically found in P, G, and Trn instance families.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/07/08/ML-21090-2-1.png" alt="Diagram showing model weights loaded from node-local NVMe storage to reduce inference pod cold-start latency" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="prerequisite-1"&gt;Prerequisite&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Before using local NVMe as a model source, verify the following:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Use GPU instances with &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/ec2/instance-types/accelerated-computing/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;local NVMe drives&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Make sure that you pre-populate model weights on the local NVMe storage of your target nodes.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="steps-to-deploy-model-from-nvme"&gt;Steps to deploy model from NVMe&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;ol type="1"&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Make sure that you pre-populate model weights on the local NVMe storage of your target nodes.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Construct the following sample YAML. For the entire deployment YAML, see the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/latest/dg/sagemaker-hyperpod-model-deployment-deploy-nvme.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Deploy models from local NVMe storage using kubectl&lt;/a&gt; 
  &lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
   &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-yaml"&gt;spec:
  modelSourceConfig:
    modelSourceType: kubernetesVolume
    kubernetes:
      volumes:
        - name: model-weights
          hostPath:
            path: /opt/dlami/nvme/&amp;lt;YOUR_MODEL&amp;gt;
            type: Directory
  worker:
    modelVolumeMount:
      name: model-weights
      mountPath: /opt/ml/model&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
  &lt;/div&gt; &lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Deploy the YAML file with kubectl command: 
  &lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
   &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;kubectl apply -f deploy_nvme_k8s_volume.yaml&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
  &lt;/div&gt; &lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Verify the deployment status: 
  &lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
   &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;kubectl describe InferenceEndpointConfig nvme-k8s-volume -n $CLUSTER_NAMESPACE&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
  &lt;/div&gt; &lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ol&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="custom-service-accounts"&gt;Custom service accounts&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;By default, inference pods use the namespace’s default ServiceAccount. For workloads that need AWS credentials (such as downloading model weights from S3 in a fallback initContainer), you can assign a custom ServiceAccount with IRSA (IAM Roles for Service Accounts) support.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="prerequisites-2"&gt;Prerequisites&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Custom service account support remains off by default. A cluster administrator must enable it before use:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;helm upgrade hyperpod-inference-operator &amp;lt;CHART_PATH&amp;gt; \
  --set enableCustomServiceAccounts=true \
  --reuse-values&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;If deployed as an Amazon Elastic Kubernetes Service (Amazon EKS) add-on, update the add-on configuration to include &lt;code&gt;enableCustomServiceAccounts: true&lt;/code&gt; in the advanced configuration settings.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="steps-to-set-up-a-custom-service-account"&gt;Steps to set up a custom service account&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;ol type="1"&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Create a Kubernetes ServiceAccount annotated with your IRSA role ARN: 
  &lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
   &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;kubectl create sa my-inference-sa -n my-namespace

kubectl annotate sa my-inference-sa -n my-namespace \
  eks.amazonaws.com/role-arn=arn:aws:iam::&amp;lt;ACCOUNT_ID&amp;gt;:role/&amp;lt;ROLE_NAME&amp;gt;&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
  &lt;/div&gt; &lt;p&gt;The specified ServiceAccount provides permissions to all containers in the inference pod (worker, init containers, and sidecars). When you annotate the ServiceAccount with &lt;code&gt;eks.amazonaws.com/role-arn&lt;/code&gt;, the pod can obtain temporary credentials for that IAM role.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Label the Service Account as user-assignable. Only ServiceAccounts with the user-assignable label can be referenced by inference endpoints. This is a security control to prevent unauthorized privilege escalation: 
  &lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
   &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;kubectl label serviceaccount my-inference-sa \
  sagemaker.amazonaws.com/user-assignable=true \
  -n my-namespace&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
  &lt;/div&gt; &lt;p&gt;Only cluster administrators should add or remove this label. Use Kubernetes RBAC to restrict who can modify ServiceAccount labels in your namespace.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Reference in InferenceEndpointConfig. Add the serviceAccountName field under spec.kubernetes of InferenceEndpointConfig CRD: 
  &lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
   &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-yaml"&gt;apiVersion: inference.sagemaker.aws.amazon.com/v1
kind: InferenceEndpointConfig
metadata:
  name: my-inference-endpoint
  namespace: my-namespace
spec:
  kubernetes:
    serviceAccountName: my-inference-sa&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
  &lt;/div&gt; &lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ol&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="validation-rules"&gt;Validation rules&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The operator validates the ServiceAccount on create and update. The operator rejects deployment with DeploymentFailed status if:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;The ServiceAccount doesn’t exist in the namespace.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;The ServiceAccount is missing the &lt;code&gt;sagemaker.amazonaws.com/user-assignable=true&lt;/code&gt; label.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;The ServiceAccount references the operator’s own system ServiceAccount.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="security-best-practices"&gt;Security best practices&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Create a dedicated ServiceAccount per inference workload. Don’t reuse across unrelated workloads.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Scope IRSA IAM roles to the specific S3 bucket and prefix containing your model weights. Avoid broad policies like AmazonS3FullAccess.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Periodically audit which ServiceAccounts carry the user-assignable label.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;For S3/FSx model sources, the operator provisions storage automatically without requiring a custom service account.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="conclusion"&gt;Conclusion&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In this post, we walked through five capabilities now available in SageMaker HyperPod inference: multi-tier data capture for auditing and model improvement, direct deployment from Hugging Face Hub, local NVMe model loading for faster cold starts, automated Route 53 DNS for custom domains, and pod-level IAM through custom service accounts. These features provide additional tools and automation to support your workflow from model deployment to production. To get started, update your Inference Operator to v3.2 and try adding a &lt;code&gt;dataCapture&lt;/code&gt; or &lt;code&gt;dnsConfig&lt;/code&gt; section to an existing deployment. For full configuration references, see the linked documentation in each preceding section.&lt;/p&gt;

			</item>
		<item>
		<title>Introducing Claude apps gateway for AWS</title>
		<link>https://aws.amazon.com/blogs/machine-learning/introducing-claude-apps-gateway-for-aws/</link>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Dani Mitchell]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Wed, 08 Jul 2026 19:49:22 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Amazon Bedrock]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Announcements]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Intermediate (200)]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">aab7835c24ac9ea0859bc1a5eac84f01ddb8a05d</guid>

					<description>Today, we're announcing the Claude apps gateway for AWS, a self-hosted control plane that gives organizations a single point of control over access, cost, and policy for Claude Code and Claude Desktop. In this post, we show how to set up and run Claude apps gateway for AWS with Amazon Bedrock and Claude Platform on AWS.</description>
										<content:encoded>&lt;p&gt;Enterprises deploying Claude Code and Claude Desktop across development teams need centralized control over access, cost, and policy. At scale, this is hard to manage: each developer needs an individual credential, settings must be distributed manually, and spend is difficult to track or cap. Without a centralized control point, governance is left to whatever tooling each team can implement independently.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Today, we’re announcing the Claude apps gateway for AWS, a self-hosted control plane that gives organizations a single point of control over access, cost, and policy for Claude Code and Claude Desktop. It replaces the need to provision a separate cloud credential per developer, push settings to every laptop by hand, or stand up separate tooling to track spend. You can deploy it through Amazon Bedrock to keep data within the AWS security boundary, or through Claude Platform on AWS to get the same gateway controls with the native Claude platform experience.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div id="attachment_135115" style="width: 1290px" class="wp-caption alignnone"&gt;
 &lt;img aria-describedby="caption-attachment-135115" loading="lazy" class="size-full wp-image-135115" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/07/08/Screenshot-2026-07-08-at-2.00.31 PM.png" alt="High level overview of Claude apps gateway for AWS " width="1280" height="396"&gt;
 &lt;p id="caption-attachment-135115" class="wp-caption-text"&gt;High level overview of Claude apps gateway for AWS&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In this post, we show how to set up and run Claude apps gateway for AWS with Amazon Bedrock and Claude Platform on AWS.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="how-the-claude-apps-gateway-works"&gt;How the Claude apps gateway works&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The gateway is delivered by Anthropic inside the same &lt;a href="https://code.claude.com/docs/en/quickstart" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Claude Code CLI&lt;/a&gt; binary your developers already use. You can run it in one stateless container on your infrastructure, backed by a &lt;a href="https://www.postgresql.org/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;PostgreSQL&lt;/a&gt; database that stores short-lived sign-in state and rate-limit counters. Because the gateway and the client are built together, the &lt;code&gt;/login&lt;/code&gt; flow is gateway-aware. The client applies managed settings automatically at sign-in, and policy is enforced consistently on every request.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Onboarding and offboarding follow your existing identity workflows. To grant access, add a developer to your identity provider (IdP). To revoke it, remove them, and their session expires within the configured token lifetime (one hour by default). No long-lived secrets live on developer machines.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div id="attachment_135121" style="width: 1132px" class="wp-caption alignnone"&gt;
 &lt;img aria-describedby="caption-attachment-135121" loading="lazy" class="size-full wp-image-135121" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/07/08/Screenshot-2026-07-08-at-2.26.43 PM.png" alt="Figure 1: Claude apps gateway architecture for AWS" width="1122" height="524"&gt;
 &lt;p id="caption-attachment-135121" class="wp-caption-text"&gt;Figure 1: Claude apps gateway architecture for AWS&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The gateway handles five core responsibilities:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Identity:&lt;/strong&gt; The gateway connects to any standards-compliant OpenID Connect (OIDC) identity provider. After a developer signs in through browser single sign-on (SSO), the gateway issues a short-lived token that the CLI uses for all subsequent requests.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Policy:&lt;/strong&gt; You define managed settings once on the server. Clients receive policy at sign-in, and the gateway enforces it on every request. You can adjust allowed models, tool permissions, and default settings centrally, scoped by IdP group.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Telemetry:&lt;/strong&gt; The client stamps a usage metric for every request, and the gateway relays it over OpenTelemetry Protocol (OTLP) to a collector you configure, such as Amazon CloudWatch or Amazon Managed Service for Prometheus in your own account, or a third-party platform. You control where telemetry goes and how long it’s retained.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Routing:&lt;/strong&gt; The gateway holds your upstream credential and routes inference requests to Amazon Bedrock or Claude Platform on AWS on behalf of developers, with optional failover between AWS Regions or across multiple accounts.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Spend caps:&lt;/strong&gt; Set daily, weekly, and monthly spend limits per organization, group, or user. When a developer exceeds their cap, the gateway blocks further requests until the period resets or an admin raises the limit.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;When Claude apps gateway is used with Amazon Bedrock, inference requests go through Amazon Bedrock in the AWS Regions you configure, maintaining the same data handling and privacy controls as any other Amazon Bedrock workload in your account. When Claude apps gateway is used with Claude Platform on AWS, requests are processed by Anthropic.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="the-configuration"&gt;The configuration&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The gateway reads a single YAML file at startup. Here’s what a minimal production configuration looks like:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div style="width: 810px" class="wp-caption alignnone"&gt;
 &lt;img loading="lazy" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/07/02/ML-21305-2.jpg" alt="Minimal gateway.yaml configuration for an Amazon Bedrock deployment" width="800" height="1006"&gt;
 &lt;p class="wp-caption-text"&gt;gateway.yaml — the full configuration for an Amazon Bedrock deployment&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The file contains six sections and the secrets stay in environment variables. The Bedrock upstream uses the container’s IAM role, so there are no static credentials to manage. To route through Claude Platform on AWS instead, replace the upstreams block:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-yaml"&gt;upstreams:
  - provider: anthropicAws
    region: us-east-1
    workspace_id: wrkspc_...
    auth: {} # AWS default credential chain (IAM role)&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Model IDs are the same as the Anthropic API (&lt;code&gt;claude-sonnet-5&lt;/code&gt;, &lt;code&gt;claude-opus-4-8&lt;/code&gt;). No Amazon Bedrock ARNs or inference profiles needed.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The gateway runs as a stateless container in your private network on &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/ecs/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Elastic Container Service (Amazon ECS)&lt;/a&gt;, &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/eks/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Elastic Kubernetes Service (Amazon EKS)&lt;/a&gt;, or &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/ec2/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Elastic Compute Cloud (Amazon EC2)&lt;/a&gt;. You place it behind an internal &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/elasticloadbalancing/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Application Load Balancer&lt;/a&gt; with a Transport Layer Security (TLS) certificate from &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/certificate-manager/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS Certificate Manager&lt;/a&gt;. &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/rds/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Relational Database Service (Amazon RDS)&lt;/a&gt; for PostgreSQL stores short-lived sign-in state. Developers reach the gateway through your private network, and the gateway uses an IAM task role to call the upstream provider on their behalf.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="developer-sign-in"&gt;Developer sign-in&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Once the gateway is deployed, developers run &lt;code&gt;claude /login&lt;/code&gt;. Administrators push a managed settings file to developer machines via their device management tool that pre-fills the gateway URL, so developers see the Claude apps gateway screen directly.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div style="width: 810px" class="wp-caption alignnone"&gt;
 &lt;img loading="lazy" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/07/02/ML-21305-3.png" alt="Claude apps gateway login screen in Claude Code" width="800" height="692"&gt;
 &lt;p class="wp-caption-text"&gt;The Claude apps gateway login screen in Claude Code&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;They press Enter, and a browser opens with your corporate SSO.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div style="width: 810px" class="wp-caption alignnone"&gt;
 &lt;img loading="lazy" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/07/02/ML-21305-4.jpg" alt="Corporate single sign-on authentication screen in the browser" width="800" height="1358"&gt;
 &lt;p class="wp-caption-text"&gt;Browser SSO authentication through your identity provider&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;One sign-in, and they’re connected. The session refreshes silently in the background using OIDC refresh tokens, so developers stay authenticated across restarts without repeated browser logins. If a user is removed from the IdP, their session expires at the next refresh.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="working-with-claude-code"&gt;Working with Claude Code&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;After sign-in, developers use Claude Code exactly as they would with any other authentication method. They write code, run commands, and interact with Claude normally. The difference is invisible to them: every request is authenticated through the gateway, routed through your configured upstream, and governed by the policies you set centrally.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div style="width: 810px" class="wp-caption alignnone"&gt;
 &lt;img loading="lazy" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/07/02/ML-21305-5.png" alt="Claude Code responding to a prompt, routed through Amazon Bedrock via the gateway" width="800" height="630"&gt;
 &lt;p class="wp-caption-text"&gt;Claude Code responding to a prompt, routed through Amazon Bedrock via the gateway&lt;/p&gt;
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The &lt;code&gt;/model&lt;/code&gt; picker shows only the models your policy allows. Beyond model access, policies can control tool permissions, such as restricting file writes or web access. They can also enforce permission rules that developers cannot override locally, and push environment variables or hooks to standardize workflows across teams. Usage is attributed to each developer’s identity, and spend is tracked against their cap. If they leave the company, removing them from the IdP revokes access within the configured session lifetime.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="conclusion"&gt;Conclusion&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;With the Claude apps gateway for AWS, you can expand Claude Code and Claude Desktop adoption across your organization while managing identity, policy, and cost from one place. Identity flows through your existing IdP, policy is enforced centrally, and cost is attributed per user, with no long-lived secrets on developer machines.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Because the gateway is self-hosted, you can deploy it in any AWS Region and route inference to Amazon Bedrock or Claude Platform on AWS, including cross-Region and cross-account setups. Choose Amazon Bedrock when data must stay within the AWS security boundary, or Claude Platform on AWS for access to Anthropic’s native platform experience with AWS authentication and billing.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To get started, download the &lt;a href="https://code.claude.com/docs/en/quickstart" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Claude Code CLI&lt;/a&gt; and review the &lt;a href="https://code.claude.com/docs/en/claude-apps-gateway" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Claude apps gateway documentation&lt;/a&gt;. Send feedback to AWS re:Post for Amazon Bedrock or through your usual AWS Support contacts.&lt;/p&gt;

		<item>
		<title>Powering scientific discovery: BYOKG and GraphRAG for intelligent pharmaceutical research</title>
		<link>https://aws.amazon.com/blogs/machine-learning/powering-scientific-discovery-byokg-and-graphrag-for-intelligent-pharmaceutical-research/</link>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Jasmine Rasheed Syed]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Wed, 08 Jul 2026 16:57:12 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Advanced (300)]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Amazon Bedrock]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Amazon Neptune]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Technical How-to]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">227b85d5546f6b457720a1b61543f09b41cbda73</guid>

					<description>In this post, we explore how Graph-based Retrieval Augmented Generation (GraphRAG) is transforming scientific research by combining graph databases with generative AI. With this approach, you can accelerate discovery processes without compromising scientific integrity.</description>
										<content:encoded>&lt;p&gt;In pharmaceutical research, scientists face a fundamental challenge: accessing and connecting the vast amount of scientific knowledge scattered across disparate systems. From published literature and internal lab notes to genomics databases, critical insights remain trapped in silos, making it difficult for researchers to form comprehensive connections and generate promising hypotheses. This fragmentation slows down the drug discovery process. It also risks valuable institutional knowledge being lost as researchers transition, ultimately affecting the industry’s ability to research and develop efficiently. The need for a solution that can intelligently bridge these knowledge gaps while maintaining scientific integrity has become increasingly important.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="the-challenge-scattered-data-across-fragmented-systems"&gt;The challenge: Scattered data across fragmented systems&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;At leading pharmaceutical companies, researchers face a critical challenge in early-stage drug discovery, where traditional methods yield only a 5 percent success rate and initial screening takes over six months. Scientists struggle to connect insights buried across fragmented systems such as PubMed, internal lab notes, and genomics databases, all while racing against competitors and time constraints. The scattered nature of data leads to redundant work and missed opportunities. It also makes it difficult to trace the evidence trail needed for regulatory approval. When researchers depart, they often take valuable tacit knowledge with them, further compromising the institutional memory needed for breakthrough discoveries.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Challenges in early-stage drug discovery:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ol type="1"&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Poor success rate and time efficiency – Only 5 percent hit rate with over 6 months of screening time per attempt.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Fragmented knowledge systems – Critical insights scattered across PubMed, lab notes, and databases, leading to missed connections.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Loss of institutional memory – Valuable knowledge disappears when researchers leave, breaking continuity in research efforts.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ol&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;These challenges collectively create a significant bottleneck in the drug discovery pipeline, leading to inefficiencies, missed opportunities, and potential delays in developing life-saving treatments. Our solution addresses these bottlenecks by moving beyond traditional methods: graph-powered AI supports pharmaceutical research by creating an interconnected knowledge environment. Using &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/neptune-analytics/latest/userguide/what-is-neptune-analytics.html"&gt;Amazon Neptune Analytics&lt;/a&gt;, researchers can now ask complex questions in natural language and receive instant, evidence-backed insights drawn from a unified knowledge graph that connects everything from compound interactions to gene expressions and clinical studies. This approach doesn’t only provide answers. It reveals the complete reasoning behind each result by showing detailed citation paths and graph traversal steps. By exposing how the system navigates through interconnected research papers and data points, it makes scientific discovery more transparent and reproducible.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;By combining graph and generative AI, research scientists don’t only retrieve information. They can amplify reasoning, preserve institutional memory, and surface insights that would otherwise stay buried. It also helps them generate better hypotheses, move faster, and trust the outputs, because every insight comes with context and proof. In a field where the cost of delay is measured in both dollars and lives, this shift is more than helpful. It changes how research gets done.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In this post, we explore how Graph-based Retrieval Augmented Generation (GraphRAG) is transforming scientific research by combining graph databases with generative AI. With this approach, you can accelerate discovery processes without compromising scientific integrity.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;By integrating Amazon Neptune Analytics for high-performance graph processing with Amazon Bedrock, researchers can build sophisticated systems that not only understand complex scientific relationships but also provide intuitive natural language interfaces. The GraphRAG architecture helps enhance the quality of AI-generated responses by intelligently traversing knowledge graphs to identify relevant information paths. This makes sure that the responses are firmly anchored in verified scientific data.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;What makes this solution powerful for scientific research is its ability to understand and connect intricate relationships between entities, from plants and compounds to proteins, genes, and their associated health effects. With this comprehensive understanding, researchers can uncover insights more efficiently and make data-driven decisions with greater confidence.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="solution-overview"&gt;Solution overview&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The solution reimagines the research process through a Bring Your Own Knowledge Graph (BYOKG) approach enhanced with GraphRAG capabilities. A knowledge graph is a structured representation of information that shows relationships between different entities as a network of interconnected nodes and edges. Powered by Amazon Neptune, it integrates diverse scientific entities (plants, compounds, genes, proteins, and health effects) into a unified knowledge network that bridges data from public sources like PubMed and Gene Ontology with proprietary datasets. Automated ingestion pipelines and graph algorithms continuously enrich the graph, helping researchers uncover complex biological relationships and insights that were previously hidden across disconnected data silos.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Using Neptune Analytics and Amazon Bedrock, the solution combines graph algorithms with natural language querying to make scientific exploration both analytical and intuitive. Researchers can ask complex questions in plain English and receive evidence-based answers derived from graph traversal, complete with source citations and visual pathways. Interactive visualization tools further help enhance transparency and understanding, allowing users to explore relationships, trace hypotheses to conclusions, and validate results with clear, verifiable evidence. This accelerates discovery and strengthens scientific rigor across domains.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="solution-architecture"&gt;Solution architecture&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Our solution helps researchers quickly discover relevant medical journal articles across conditions and topics. The dataset includes the HCLS journal articles provided by the &lt;a href="https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/tools/openftlist/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;PMC Open Access Subset&lt;/a&gt; licensed with CC BY and CC0 licenses, journal metadata provided by the National Center for Biotechnology Information (NCBI) via the &lt;a href="https://biopython.org/docs/1.75/api/Bio.Entrez.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Bio.Entrez&lt;/a&gt; package, &lt;a href="https://disease-ontology.org/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Disease Ontology&lt;/a&gt; hierarchies, and ICD10 codes that have been extracted using the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/comprehend-medical/latest/dev/ontology-icd10.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;ICD-10-CM linking&lt;/a&gt; API within Amazon Comprehend Medical. Although the final dataset is provided to you, the following architecture depicts the flow used to create the dataset.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The following diagram illustrates the loading of the data to Amazon Neptune Analytics using services like Amazon Bedrock and Amazon Comprehend to extract data from medical journals.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/30/ML-19455-1.jpeg" alt="Architecture diagram showing data loaded into Amazon Neptune Analytics, with Amazon Bedrock and Amazon Comprehend Medical extracting data from medical journals" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The following image represents the final graph, which contains these node types:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;disease&lt;/strong&gt;: Represents a disease within the &lt;a href="https://github.com/DiseaseOntology/HumanDiseaseOntology/tree/main" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Disease Ontology&lt;/a&gt;. The Disease Ontology provides a mapping to help us understand which diseases are subclasses of other diseases.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;author&lt;/strong&gt;: Represents an author of a particular journal.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;journal&lt;/strong&gt;: Represents a journal.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;journalChunk&lt;/strong&gt;: Represents a chunk of a given journal. Chunks were determined using the default chunking strategy provided through Amazon Bedrock Knowledge Bases.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;icd10&lt;/strong&gt;: Represents an &lt;a href="https://www.cms.gov/medicare/coding-billing/icd-10-codes" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;ICD-10&lt;/a&gt; code, which is a standardized classification of medical issues. Edges between &lt;code&gt;icd10&lt;/code&gt; nodes and &lt;code&gt;journal&lt;/code&gt; and &lt;code&gt;journalChunk&lt;/code&gt; nodes were created using the Amazon Comprehend Medical &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/comprehend-medical/latest/dev/ontology-icd10.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;ICD-10-CM linking&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/30/ML-19455-2.jpeg" alt="Knowledge graph showing disease, author, journal, journalChunk, and icd10 node types connected by edges" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Because we’re using our own graph data model, we use the &lt;a href="https://github.com/awslabs/graphrag-toolkit/tree/main/byokg-rag" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;BYOKG-RAG&lt;/a&gt; toolkit to implement natural language querying over the graph. The following diagram illustrates the components of BYOKG.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/30/ML-19455-3.jpeg" alt="Diagram showing the components of the BYOKG-RAG toolkit" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="prerequisites"&gt;Prerequisites&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Before getting started, make sure you have the following prerequisites:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;AWS Command Line Interface (AWS CLI) version 2.11.0 or later installed and configured (&lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/cli/latest/userguide/getting-started-install.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;installation guide&lt;/a&gt;)&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Access to the following AWS services: 
  &lt;ul&gt; 
   &lt;li&gt;Amazon Neptune Analytics&lt;/li&gt; 
   &lt;li&gt;Amazon Bedrock (Claude 4.5 Sonnet model)&lt;/li&gt; 
   &lt;li&gt;Amazon SageMaker&lt;/li&gt; 
   &lt;li&gt;Amazon Simple Storage Service (Amazon S3)&lt;/li&gt; 
   &lt;li&gt;Amazon Comprehend Medical&lt;/li&gt; 
  &lt;/ul&gt; &lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;IAM role with the following permissions: 
  &lt;ul&gt; 
   &lt;li&gt;NeptuneAnalyticsFullAccess&lt;/li&gt; 
   &lt;li&gt;AWSServiceRoleForAmazonNeptuneAnalytics&lt;/li&gt; 
   &lt;li&gt;AmazonS3ReadOnlyAccess&lt;/li&gt; 
   &lt;li&gt;AmazonBedrockFullAccess&lt;/li&gt; 
   &lt;li&gt;ComprehendMedicalFullAccess&lt;/li&gt; 
  &lt;/ul&gt; &lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Python 3.9 or later&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;graphrag_toolkit version 1.0.0 or later&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Jupyter Notebook environment&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="solution-cost-overview"&gt;Solution cost overview&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Cost approximation (per hour) for running this demo:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Neptune Analytics graph, 16 mNCU, no standby, public connectivity – $0.48/hour.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;SageMaker Jupyter notebook, t3.medium with 5 GB of EBS volume – $0.05/hour for compute plus $0.70/hour for storage.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;S3 storage, standard, 161 MB – 0.161 GB × $0.023 = $0.0037 per month.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Amazon Bedrock – The cost for Amazon Bedrock depends on model usage and token consumption. For the most up-to-date information, see the &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/bedrock/pricing/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;pricing page&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="setting-up-neptune-analytics"&gt;Setting up Neptune Analytics&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Let’s begin implementing your GraphRAG solution by setting up Neptune Analytics. The following steps will guide you through data import, graph creation, and notebook configuration to build your knowledge graph foundation:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ol type="1"&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Create an S3 bucket: &lt;code&gt;aws s3 mb s3://amzn-s3-bucket-name&lt;/code&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Copy the dataset into your own buckets using the AWS CLI: 
  &lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
   &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;aws s3 sync s3://aws-neptune-customer-samples-us-east-1/sample-notebooks/vector-graph-hybrid-search/graph-data/ s3://amzn-s3-bucket-name/&amp;lt;YOUR PREFIX&amp;gt;&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
  &lt;/div&gt; &lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Create a Neptune Analytics graph using the &lt;code&gt;CreateGraphUsingImportTask&lt;/code&gt; API to import from the Amazon S3 location you copied to in the first step. For details on how to do this, see the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/neptune-analytics/latest/userguide/bulk-import-create-from-s3.html#use-createGraphUsingImportTask-to-import" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Neptune Analytics User Guide&lt;/a&gt;. Set the minimum and maximum provisioned memory to 16.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;While the graph is creating, create a Neptune Notebook associated with the graph. The notebook makes it straightforward to query and interact with the graph, as well as set up and run the GraphRAG Toolkit. For details on how to create a Neptune Notebook, see the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/neptune-analytics/latest/userguide/create-notebook-console.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Neptune Analytics User Guide&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Download the &lt;a href="https://gist.github.com/jagadishkmar/dc42495c52d89d128d5e88134d47023e" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;sample notebook&lt;/a&gt;, and upload it to your Jupyter environment.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ol&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/30/ML-19455-4.jpeg" alt="Sample GraphRAG notebook uploaded to the SageMaker Jupyter environment" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="implementation-steps-building-a-modular-graphrag-system-with-the-graphrag-toolkit-and-amazon-bedrock"&gt;Implementation steps: Building a modular GraphRAG system with the GraphRAG Toolkit and Amazon Bedrock&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In this &lt;a href="https://gist.github.com/jagadishkmar/dc42495c52d89d128d5e88134d47023e" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;notebook&lt;/a&gt;, we demonstrate a modular approach to building a Retrieval Augmented Generation (RAG) system over a healthcare knowledge graph, using the &lt;a href="https://github.com/awslabs/graphrag-toolkit" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;graphrag-toolkit&lt;/a&gt; Python package and the Amazon Bedrock Anthropic Claude 4.5 Sonnet model. This solution supports natural language querying and entity linking within a knowledge graph, combining advanced language model generation with structured graph data retrieval.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="key-components"&gt;Key components&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;ol type="1"&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Language model initialization&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;br&gt; We begin by initializing the Amazon Bedrock-based language model generator that powers our natural language responses.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ol&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-python"&gt;from graphrag_toolkit.byokg_rag.llm import BedrockGenerator

def init_llm_generator(model_name='us.anthropic.claude-3-5-sonnet-20240620-v1:0', region_name='us-west-2'):
    return BedrockGenerator(
        model_name=model_name,
        region_name=region_name
    )

llm_generator = init_llm_generator()&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;ol start="2" type="1"&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Knowledge graph linker setup&lt;/strong&gt;The &lt;code&gt;KGLinker&lt;/code&gt; is initialized by passing the graph store and the language model generator. It acts as the core interface to query the graph and generate answers.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ol&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-python"&gt;from graphrag_toolkit.byokg_rag.graph_connectors import KGLinker

def init_kg_linker(graph_store, llm_generator):
    return KGLinker(graph_store=graph_store, llm_generator=llm_generator)

kg_linker = init_kg_linker(graph_store, llm_generator)&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;ol start="3" type="1"&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Generating responses from queries&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;br&gt; With the &lt;code&gt;kg_linker&lt;/code&gt;, we can pose natural language questions and obtain generated responses augmented by the knowledge graph context.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ol&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-python"&gt;def generate_kg_response(kg_linker, question, schema, graph_context="Not provided. Use the above schema to understand the graph."):
    return kg_linker.generate_response(
        question=question,
        schema=schema,
        graph_context=graph_context
    )

# Example usage
response = generate_kg_response(kg_linker, question, schema)
print(response)&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;ol start="4" type="1"&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Entity linking for enhanced retrieval&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;br&gt; To improve information extraction and link natural language text to graph entities, we use a fuzzy string index combined with the &lt;code&gt;EntityLinker&lt;/code&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ol&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-python"&gt;from graphrag_toolkit.byokg_rag.indexing import FuzzyStringIndex
from graphrag_toolkit.byokg_rag.graph_retrievers import EntityLinker

def init_and_link_entities(graph_store, artifacts):
    string_index = FuzzyStringIndex()
    string_index.add(graph_store.nodes())
    retriever = string_index.as_entity_matcher()
    entity_linker = EntityLinker(retriever=retriever)

    linked_entities = entity_linker.link(artifacts["entity-extraction"], return_dict=False)
    linked_answers = entity_linker.link(artifacts["draft-answer-generation"], return_dict=False)

    return entity_linker, linked_entities, linked_answers

entity_linker, linked_entities, linked_answers = init_and_link_entities(graph_store, artifacts)&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="summary"&gt;Summary&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This modular structure cleanly separates the following components:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Large language model (LLM) initialization (using Amazon Bedrock).&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Knowledge graph interfacing (&lt;code&gt;KGLinker&lt;/code&gt;).&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;NLP (natural language querying).&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Entity linking on graph nodes.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The modular structure allows flexible experimentation and straightforward extension for different domains or datasets.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The integration of a fuzzy string matcher facilitates robust entity recognition, which is important in noisy or complex healthcare data contexts.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;By combining the Amazon Bedrock advanced language models with structured graph querying and linking, this solution forms a powerful foundation for context-aware question answering and information retrieval over knowledge graphs.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="solution-benefits-and-performance-metrics"&gt;Solution benefits and performance metrics&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The following key performance indicators from our implementation of the solution demonstrate how this GraphRAG solution can create measurable value and competitive advantage for pharmaceutical research organizations:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Research timeline acceleration –&lt;/strong&gt; GraphRAG reduces research cycles from six months to three weeks, delivering an 87 percent efficiency boost. This supports rapid hypothesis testing and faster scientific breakthroughs.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Success rate optimization –&lt;/strong&gt;&amp;nbsp;GraphRAG reduced research cycles in our implementation from six months to three weeks. The solution is powered by advanced graph algorithms. Cross-domain analysis facilitates more effective research directions and optimal resource deployment.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Workflow efficiency gains –&lt;/strong&gt; Key metrics show measurable improvements in our tests: 70 percent reduction in review time, 85 percent faster data access, and 90 percent enhanced knowledge use. Teams can focus on strategic research priorities.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Data-driven validation –&lt;/strong&gt;&amp;nbsp;Advanced tracking and visualization facilitate full research transparency. Clear data pathways strengthen regulatory compliance and improve scientific communication.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Intelligent knowledge integration –&lt;/strong&gt;&amp;nbsp;The system efficiently scales and integrates new data sources with minimal resource impact. Enhanced collaboration preserves vital institutional knowledge.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Industry leadership enablement –&lt;/strong&gt;&amp;nbsp;Accelerated innovation cycles maintain scientific rigor while speeding industry entry. This helps create sustainable competitive advantages and industry leadership positions.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="clean-up"&gt;Clean up&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To avoid incurring additional charges, clean up the resources created in this post.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Delete the Neptune Analytics graph by following these &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/neptune-analytics/latest/userguide/managing-deleting.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;steps&lt;/a&gt; or by running the following CLI command.&lt;br&gt; &lt;strong&gt;Note:&lt;/strong&gt; Replace g-sample with the graph created.&lt;br&gt; &lt;code&gt;aws neptune-graph delete-graph --graph-id g-sample&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Delete the graph notebook created in the AWS Management Console by selecting the instance, choosing the A&lt;strong&gt;ctions&lt;/strong&gt; menu, and selecting the &lt;strong&gt;Delete&lt;/strong&gt; option.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/30/ML-19455-5.png" alt="Deleting the Neptune graph notebook from the SageMaker console" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Delete the S3 bucket created by following these &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/AmazonS3/latest/userguide/delete-bucket.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;steps&lt;/a&gt; or by running the CLI command.&lt;br&gt; &lt;strong&gt;Note:&lt;/strong&gt; Replace amzn-s3-bucket-name with the name of the bucket created.&lt;br&gt; &lt;code&gt;aws s3 rb s3://amzn-s3-bucket-name --force&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt;
