			</item>
		<item>
		<title>Build a unified semantic layer across datasets with multi-dataset Topics in Amazon Quick</title>
		<link>https://aws.amazon.com/blogs/machine-learning/build-a-unified-semantic-layer-across-datasets-with-multi-dataset-topics-in-amazon-quick/</link>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Emily Zhu]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Tue, 07 Jul 2026 17:07:22 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Amazon Quick Sight]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Amazon Quick Suite]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Announcements]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Intermediate (200)]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">7c77891b2ed3837eb4c5bcc52c5857f4453a2689</guid>

					<description>In this post, we walk through how multi-dataset Topics work, explain how the chat agent uses defined relationships to generate cross-dataset queries, and demonstrate an end-to-end implementation using a retail analytics scenario in Quick Sight.</description>
										<content:encoded>&lt;p&gt;&lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/quick/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Quick&lt;/a&gt; is an AI-powered unified intelligence service that connects structured data and unstructured enterprise content so teams can explore, analyze, and act from one place. Amazon Quick Sight, the business intelligence (BI) capability within Amazon Quick, delivers interactive dashboards, natural language querying, pixel-perfect reports, machine learning (ML)-driven insights, and embedded analytics. Topics in Quick function as the semantic layer that business users can use to ask questions in natural language and get answers directly from their data.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Until now, organizations modeled their semantic layers by creating enriched datasets and associating them one-to-one with topics. Also, when Quick Sight authors build analysis, one visual could be sourced from only one dataset. Quick Sight represents a dataset as a single flattened table. If a customer’s data source contains multiple tables, Quick Sight required customers to define the dataset by joining the source tables into a single table in Quick Sight data preparation. This one big denormalized table approach was initially designed to provide better performance by avoiding run-time joins. It worked well for straightforward datasets, which made up the majority of use cases during the early stages of Quick Sight. Today, we’re evolving this model. With &lt;strong&gt;multi-dataset Topics (public preview)&lt;/strong&gt;, you can now add up to 12 datasets to a single topic and define relationships between them. The Quick chat agent automatically traverses those relationships when answering questions. The AI engine interprets user intent, identifies which datasets contain the relevant columns, constructs the appropriate SQL joins based on your defined relationships, and returns a unified answer. Your data stays normalized, your governance stays centralized, and your business users get richer answers without understanding the underlying schema. The same multi-dataset topic can be used for building the analysis or answering questions using the chat agent.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In this post, we walk through how multi-dataset Topics work, explain how the chat agent uses defined relationships to generate cross-dataset queries, and demonstrate an end-to-end implementation using a retail analytics scenario in Quick Sight.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="how-multi-dataset-topics-work"&gt;How multi-dataset Topics work&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;A Topic in Quick is the semantic layer between raw data and business users. It encapsulates the metadata, business rules, relationships, and context that the AI-powered natural language query (NLQ) engine uses to interpret natural language questions and translate them into precise analytical queries.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;With multi-dataset Topics, this semantic layer now spans multiple datasets connected through explicitly defined relationships. The following diagram illustrates the end-to-end flow from data sources through to consumption.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/30/ML-21288-1.jpg" alt="Multi-dataset Topics architecture flow from data sources through enriched datasets to a topic with relationships and custom instructions, consumed through chat and analysis" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;Figure 1: Multi-dataset Topics architecture flow showing how data sources feed into enriched datasets, which are combined in a topic with relationships and custom instructions, then consumed through chat and analysis.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The architecture consists of four layers:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;1. Data sources.&lt;/strong&gt; Your normalized datasets connect to Quick Sight from supported sources. During private preview, multi-dataset Topics support SPICE (Super-fast, Parallel, In-memory Calculation Engine) datasets. Public preview expands support to Direct Query against &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/redshift/"&gt;Amazon Redshift&lt;/a&gt;, &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/athena/"&gt;Amazon Athena&lt;/a&gt;, &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/s3/features/tables/"&gt;Amazon S3 Tables&lt;/a&gt;, &lt;a href="https://www.snowflake.com/en/"&gt;Snowflake&lt;/a&gt;, and &lt;a href="https://www.databricks.com/resources/ebook/the-data-lakehouse-for-dummies?scid=7018Y000001Fi0MQAS&amp;amp;utm_medium=paid+search&amp;amp;utm_source=google&amp;amp;utm_campaign=14636097724&amp;amp;utm_adgroup=127320330459&amp;amp;utm_content=ebook&amp;amp;utm_offer=the-data-lakehouse-for-dummies&amp;amp;utm_ad=726054814749&amp;amp;utm_term=databricks&amp;amp;gad_source=1&amp;amp;gad_campaignid=14636097724&amp;amp;gbraid=0AAAAABYBeAidfPX0QrlDyY32eLh6axSVB&amp;amp;gclid=CjwKCAjw0o3SBhBVEiwAh28-jXi2cdiDLoB7bwK9rzv587bG5Hz6kzvC_5ZksfXU4NGxZLDl4idRTxoCFH4QAvD_BwE"&gt;Databricks&lt;/a&gt;. You can’t combine SPICE and Direct Query datasets within a single topic as of today.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;2. Dataset enrichment with or without catalogs.&lt;/strong&gt; Each dataset is independently enriched with semantic metadata that improves NLQ accuracy. Enrichment includes column descriptions (explaining what each field represents), synonyms (alternative terms business users might use), semantic types (City, State, Currency, Date), calculated fields, and field exclusions. The NLQ engine uses this metadata to map natural language terms to the correct columns. You can now create enriched datasets in Quick or bring them in using catalogs from &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/glue/"&gt;AWS Glue&lt;/a&gt; (AWS Glue Data Catalog) and Databricks Unity Catalog. For example, when a user asks about “headcount,” the engine matches a defined synonym to the appropriate column.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;3. Multi-dataset topic.&lt;/strong&gt; The topic serves as the unified container that brings datasets together. It holds:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Datasets (up to 12 in preview).&lt;/strong&gt; Each dataset maintains its own enrichment and connects to its own data source.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Relationships.&lt;/strong&gt; Join keys defined between dataset pairs tell the NLQ engine how tables relate. You upload a JSON file that maps columns between datasets (for example, a fact table’s foreign key joins to a dimension table’s primary key).&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Custom instructions.&lt;/strong&gt; Persistent natural language rules that guide the AI in interpreting domain-specific terminology. These handle disambiguation, custom date logic (fiscal year boundaries), and business definitions.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Permissions.&lt;/strong&gt; Owners can modify the topic. Viewers can ask questions and use the topic in analysis but can’t change its configuration.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;4. Consumption.&lt;/strong&gt; Business users interact with the topic through multiple surfaces:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Chat.&lt;/strong&gt; Users ask natural language questions in the chat interface. The NLQ engine parses the question, identifies relevant columns across datasets, constructs SQL with appropriate joins, and returns the answer as a table or visualization.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Analysis sheets.&lt;/strong&gt; Authors build visuals using fields from multiple datasets within the topic. Calculated fields can reference columns spanning different datasets.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Cross-dataset calculations.&lt;/strong&gt; You can create calculated fields that combine measures and dimensions from different datasets, and the engine handles the underlying join logic.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="how-the-chat-processes-cross-dataset-questions"&gt;How the chat processes cross-dataset questions&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;When a business user asks a natural language question that spans multiple datasets, the NLQ engine performs the following steps:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;1. Intent parsing.&lt;/strong&gt; The engine identifies which columns map to the user’s terms by matching against column names, descriptions, and synonyms from the enrichment metadata. It determines which datasets contain the relevant measures and dimensions.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;2. Relationship traversal.&lt;/strong&gt; Using the defined join keys, the engine determines the shortest join path between the identified datasets. It follows the relationship graph to connect fact tables to the necessary dimension tables.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;3. SQL generation.&lt;/strong&gt; The engine constructs a SQL query with the appropriate JOIN clauses, aggregation (SUM for “total”), and GROUP BY for the requested dimensions.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;4. Result presentation.&lt;/strong&gt; The answer is returned as a visualization or table, with the generated SQL available for inspection.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This process is transparent. Users can view the generated SQL to verify that the correct datasets and joins were used. The richer your enrichment metadata and custom instructions, the more accurately the engine interprets ambiguous questions.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="worked-example-show-total-sales-by-customer-segment-and-store-region"&gt;Worked example: Show total sales by customer segment and store region&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Consider the question: “Show total sales by customer segment and store region.”&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Step 1 – Intent parsing.&lt;/strong&gt; The engine maps:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;“total sales” → &lt;code&gt;SUM(SALES_FACT.SALE_AMOUNT)&lt;/code&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;“customer segment” → &lt;code&gt;CUSTOMER_DIM.SEGMENT&lt;/code&gt; (matched via synonym “segment”).&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;“store region” → &lt;code&gt;STORE_DIM.REGION&lt;/code&gt; (matched via synonym “region”).&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Step 2 – Relationship traversal.&lt;/strong&gt; The engine identifies that answering this question requires three datasets: SALES_FACT, CUSTOMER_DIM, and STORE_DIM. Using the defined relationships:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;code&gt;SALES_FACT.CUSTOMER_ID&lt;/code&gt; → &lt;code&gt;CUSTOMER_DIM.CUSTOMER_ID&lt;/code&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;code&gt;SALES_FACT.STORE_ID&lt;/code&gt; → &lt;code&gt;STORE_DIM.STORE_ID&lt;/code&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;It constructs a two-hop join path through SALES_FACT as the hub.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Step 3 – SQL generation.&lt;/strong&gt; The engine produces:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-sql"&gt;SELECT c.SEGMENT AS customer_segment, s.REGION AS store_region, SUM(f.SALE_AMOUNT) AS total_sales
FROM SALES_FACT f
JOIN CUSTOMER_DIM c ON f.CUSTOMER_ID = c.CUSTOMER_ID
JOIN STORE_DIM s ON f.STORE_ID = s.STORE_ID
GROUP BY c.SEGMENT, s.REGION
ORDER BY total_sales DESC&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Step 4 – Result presentation.&lt;/strong&gt; The answer renders a visual or table with all details. The user can inspect the generated SQL using the Explanation feature.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="feature-availability"&gt;Feature availability&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Multi-dataset Topics are launching progressively. The following table summarizes feature availability for Quick Sight BI visual building. To use a Topic in Quick chat agent, no such limitations apply.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Legend:&lt;/strong&gt; Yes = Available | No = Not Available | Planned = Planned for future release&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/30/ML-21288-2.jpg" alt="Table summarizing multi-dataset Topics feature availability across private preview, public preview, and general availability milestones" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="solution-overview"&gt;Solution overview&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For this walkthrough, we build a multi-dataset topic for a retail analytics scenario at AnyCompany. The scenario uses five datasets modeled in a star schema pattern.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The following diagram illustrates the star schema relationship pattern used in this implementation.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/30/ML-21288-3.jpg" alt="Star schema data model with SALES_FACT as the central fact table joined to CUSTOMER_DIM, PRODUCT_DIM, STORE_DIM, and DATE_DIM dimension tables" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;Figure 2: Star schema data model showing SALES_FACT as the central fact table, with dimension tables CUSTOMER_DIM, PRODUCT_DIM, STORE_DIM, and DATE_DIM connected via join keys.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The datasets include:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;SALES_FACT.&lt;/strong&gt; Transaction-level sales data including amounts, quantities, dates, and foreign keys to dimension tables.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;RETURN_FACT.&lt;/strong&gt; Product return records with quantities, reasons, and dates.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;CUSTOMER_DIM.&lt;/strong&gt; Customer attributes including name, segment, loyalty tier, and geographic region.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;PRODUCT_DIM.&lt;/strong&gt; Product catalog with category, brand, cost, and supplier information.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;STORE_DIM.&lt;/strong&gt; Store locations with city, state, square footage, employee count, and revenue targets.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="prerequisites"&gt;Prerequisites&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To follow along with this post, confirm you have the following in place:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;An AWS account with Amazon Quick Enterprise Edition enabled.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Author or Admin role in Amazon Quick.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Datasets representing a star schema (fact tables and dimension tables), loaded into SPICE or accessible through a supported Direct Query source.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Permissions to create topics and manage datasets.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Familiarity with basic data modeling concepts (star schema, fact/dimension tables, join keys).&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="create-and-enrich-each-dataset"&gt;Create and enrich each dataset&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Enrichment bridges the gap between technical column names and how business users actually talk about data. A column named &lt;code&gt;TXN_DT&lt;/code&gt; means nothing to a marketing analyst who asks about ‘purchase date’ or ‘order date’. By adding descriptions, synonyms, and semantic types, you teach the NLQ engine your organization’s vocabulary. This investment pays off across every question users ask. It is the foundation that makes multi-dataset Topics accurate rather than only functional.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For each dataset, complete the following:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;From the Quick console’s left pane, under Quick Sight choose &lt;strong&gt;Data&lt;/strong&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Select a dataset and choose &lt;strong&gt;Edit&lt;/strong&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Select the output tab, and for each column, add descriptions, synonyms, and semantic types.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/30/ML-21288-4.jpg" alt="Quick Sight dataset edit view showing column descriptions, synonyms, and semantic types on the output tab" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Exclude internal or unused fields such as surrogate keys, audit timestamps, and extract, transform, and load (ETL) flags.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Choose &lt;strong&gt;Save and publish&lt;/strong&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The following table shows sample enrichment for the STORE_DIM dataset.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;table border="1px" width="100%" cellpadding="10px"&gt; 
 &lt;tbody&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Column&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Description&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Synonyms&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Semantic Type&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;STORE_ID&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Unique identifier for each retail store&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Store Number, Store Code, Shop ID&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Identifier&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;LOCATION&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;City where the store is located&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;City, Store City, Town, Area&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;City&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;REGION&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;US state where the store operates&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;State, Territory, Market&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;State&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;DAILY_TARGET&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Expected daily sales revenue target in USD&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Daily Goal, Daily Quota&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Currency&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;EMPLOYEE_COUNT&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Number of employees at the store&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Headcount, Staff Count, Team Size&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Count&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;STORE_SIZE_SQFT&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Retail floor area in square feet&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Square Footage, Floor Area&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Measurement&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;OPENING_DATE&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Date the store first opened&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Launch Date, Start Date&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Date&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
 &lt;/tbody&gt; 
&lt;/table&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Repeat this process for each dataset. For detailed instructions, refer to &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quick/latest/userguide/dataset-enrichment.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Dataset enrichment&lt;/a&gt; in the Amazon Quick Sight User Guide.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="create-a-topic-with-multiple-datasets"&gt;Create a topic with multiple datasets&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;With your datasets enriched, create a topic that combines them into a single semantic layer.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ol type="1"&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;From the Quick console’s left pane, under Quick Sight choose &lt;strong&gt;Data&lt;/strong&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Navigate to the &lt;strong&gt;Topics&lt;/strong&gt; tab and choose &lt;strong&gt;Create topic&lt;/strong&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Enter a topic name (for example, “Retail Sales Analytics”) and a description.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Choose &lt;strong&gt;Add dataset&lt;/strong&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Select all relevant datasets (SALES_FACT, RETURN_FACT, CUSTOMER_DIM, PRODUCT_DIM, STORE_DIM).&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Choose &lt;strong&gt;Create&lt;/strong&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ol&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/30/ML-21288-5.jpg" alt="Quick Sight Topics tab showing the Create topic option" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/30/ML-21288-6.jpg" alt="Create topic dialog with the five retail datasets added to the topic" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="define-relationships-between-datasets"&gt;Define relationships between datasets&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Relationships tell the NLQ engine how to join datasets when a question spans multiple tables. You define relationships using a JSON configuration file that specifies join keys between dataset pairs.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ol type="1"&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;In the topic editor, navigate to the &lt;strong&gt;Relationships&lt;/strong&gt; tab.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Choose &lt;strong&gt;Upload file&lt;/strong&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Upload a JSON file defining your relationships (refer to the following example).&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;After uploading, select a join to verify the relationship mapping.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;To edit, choose &lt;strong&gt;Edit&lt;/strong&gt;, update join columns, and choose &lt;strong&gt;Save&lt;/strong&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ol&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/30/ML-21288-7.jpg" alt="Relationships tab in the topic editor with the Upload file option" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/30/ML-21288-8.jpg" alt="Uploaded relationship file showing the defined dataset joins" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/30/ML-21288-9.jpg" alt="Selected join displaying the mapped columns between two datasets" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/30/ML-21288-10.jpg" alt="Editing a join to update the join columns between datasets" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The following JSON shows a star schema configuration of the relationship file:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-json"&gt;{
  "datasetPairs": [
    {
      "datasetLeft": { "datasetName": "SALES_FACT", "joinColumnNames": ["CUSTOMER_ID"] },
      "datasetRight": { "datasetName": "CUSTOMER_DIM", "joinColumnNames": ["CUSTOMER_ID"] }
    },
    {
      "datasetLeft": { "datasetName": "SALES_FACT", "joinColumnNames": ["PRODUCT_ID"] },
      "datasetRight": { "datasetName": "PRODUCT_DIM", "joinColumnNames": ["PRODUCT_ID"] }
    },
    {
      "datasetLeft": { "datasetName": "SALES_FACT", "joinColumnNames": ["STORE_ID"] },
      "datasetRight": { "datasetName": "STORE_DIM", "joinColumnNames": ["STORE_ID"] }
    },
    {
      "datasetLeft": { "datasetName": "RETURN_FACT", "joinColumnNames": ["PRODUCT_ID"] },
      "datasetRight": { "datasetName": "PRODUCT_DIM", "joinColumnNames": ["PRODUCT_ID"] }
    }
  ]
}&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Best practice:&lt;/strong&gt; Model your datasets in a star schema with one or more central fact tables joined to shared dimension tables. Avoid circular joins and many-to-many relationships.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="add-custom-instructions"&gt;Add custom instructions&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Custom instructions act as persistent business rules that the NLQ engine applies to every question. Without them, the engine interprets terms literally. For example, ‘this year’ defaults to the calendar year. However, if your organization runs on a fiscal year starting April 1, you need an instruction to override that default. Similarly, business-specific metrics like ‘return rate’ require explicit definitions so the engine calculates them consistently across all users.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ol type="1"&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;In the topic editor, navigate to the &lt;strong&gt;Custom instructions&lt;/strong&gt; tab.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Choose &lt;strong&gt;Edit&lt;/strong&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Add your instructions (the following examples).&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Choose &lt;strong&gt;Save changes&lt;/strong&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ol&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/30/ML-21288-11.jpg" alt="Custom instructions tab in the topic editor" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/30/ML-21288-12.jpg" alt="Custom instructions entered as natural language business rules" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Example custom instructions:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;“Fiscal year starts April 1. Interpret ‘this year’ using fiscal year boundaries.”&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;“Active customers means customers with at least one purchase in the last 90 days.”&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;“When ‘sales’ is mentioned without qualification, default to &lt;code&gt;net_sales_amount&lt;/code&gt;.”&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;“Return rate = count of returned items (RETURN_FACT) / total items sold (SALES_FACT), as a percentage.”&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="publish-and-share-the-topic"&gt;Publish and share the topic&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;After configuring your topic, share it with business users.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;From the topic page, choose &lt;strong&gt;Publish&lt;/strong&gt;, then choose &lt;strong&gt;Publish&lt;/strong&gt; again on the popup.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;From the topic page, select the ellipsis menu and choose &lt;strong&gt;Share&lt;/strong&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Search for and add specific users or groups.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Set permission levels (Owner or Viewer) and choose &lt;strong&gt;Done&lt;/strong&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;div style="width: 640px;" class="wp-video"&gt;
 &lt;video class="wp-video-shortcode" id="video-134577-2" width="640" height="360" preload="metadata" controls="controls"&gt;
  &lt;source type="video/mp4" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/artifacts/DBSBlogs/ML-21218/publish+and+share.mp4?_=2"&gt;
 &lt;/video&gt;
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;table border="1px" width="100%" cellpadding="10px"&gt; 
 &lt;tbody&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Permission Level&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Can Ask Questions&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Can Modify Topic&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Can use in Analysis&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Owner&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Yes&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Yes&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Yes&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Viewer&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Yes&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;No&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Yes&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
 &lt;/tbody&gt; 
&lt;/table&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Viewers can immediately start asking natural language questions. Quick Sight enforces row-level security (RLS) and column-level security (CLS) at the dataset level, so access controls are preserved through the topic’s semantic layer.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="use-the-topic-in-analysis-and-chat"&gt;Use the topic in analysis and chat&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="using-in-analysis-sheets"&gt;Using in analysis sheets&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Navigate to the topic and choose &lt;strong&gt;Create analysis&lt;/strong&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Select &lt;strong&gt;Interactive sheet&lt;/strong&gt; and choose &lt;strong&gt;Create&lt;/strong&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Select fields from multiple datasets and add them to a visual.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Create calculated fields that reference columns from different datasets.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;div style="width: 640px;" class="wp-video"&gt;
 &lt;video class="wp-video-shortcode" id="video-134577-3" width="640" height="360" preload="metadata" controls="controls"&gt;
  &lt;source type="video/mp4" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/artifacts/DBSBlogs/ML-21218/create+analysis.mp4?_=3"&gt;
 &lt;/video&gt;
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="using-in-chat-nlq"&gt;Using in chat (NLQ)&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Navigate to the topic and choose the chat icon.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Your topic is automatically available as the context.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Ask natural language questions that span multiple datasets: 
  &lt;ul&gt; 
   &lt;li&gt;&lt;em&gt;“Summarize topic?”&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/li&gt; 
   &lt;li&gt;&lt;em&gt;“Return analysis”&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/li&gt; 
   &lt;li&gt;&lt;em&gt;“Show return rate trend as % of quantity?”&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/li&gt; 
  &lt;/ul&gt; &lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;The chat agent uses your defined relationships, descriptions, synonyms, and custom instructions to generate accurate answers.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Explore results and explanation. Notice how the SQL shows joins from multiple datasets.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The generated SQL demonstrates multi-dataset joins in action. For example, a question about “sales by region” produces SQL that joins SALES_FACT to STORE_DIM on STORE_ID and groups by the REGION column.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div style="width: 640px;" class="wp-video"&gt;
 &lt;video class="wp-video-shortcode" id="video-134577-4" width="640" height="360" preload="metadata" controls="controls"&gt;
  &lt;source type="video/mp4" src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/artifacts/DBSBlogs/ML-21218/qna.mp4?_=4"&gt;
 &lt;/video&gt;
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="clean-up"&gt;Clean up&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To avoid incurring ongoing charges, delete the AWS resources, such as &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/iam/"&gt;AWS Identity and Access Management (IAM)&lt;/a&gt; roles, Quick Sight data sources, and policies, that you created as part of this experimentation. For instructions, see the service documentation.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="conclusion"&gt;Conclusion&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In this post, you learned how to use multi-dataset Topics in Amazon Quick Sight to build a unified semantic layer across multiple normalized datasets without pre-joining or duplicating data. By defining relationships through join keys, enriching each dataset with semantic metadata, and adding custom instructions, you give the NLQ engine the context it needs to answer cross-dataset natural language questions accurately.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This feature preserves your star schema design, reduces data duplication, and gives administrators a single governed surface to manage business definitions across all related datasets. Business users get richer, cross-domain answers through chat and analysis without understanding joins or SQL.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To get started, create a topic and add your related datasets today. For the full configuration reference, see Working with Amazon Quick Sight Topics in the documentation. For additional discussions and help getting answers to your questions, check out the &lt;a href="https://community.amazonquicksight.com/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Quick Community&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Refer to the &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/blogs/machine-learning/data-modeling-best-practices-for-amazon-quick-sight-multi-dataset-relationships/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Data Modeling Best Practices for Amazon Quick Sight Multi-Dataset Relationships&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt; blog post for a detailed look at different scenarios and how to model those using Quick Sight.&lt;/p&gt;

		<title>Built to bounce back: How Azure resiliency evolved</title>

		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Rochak Mittal, Amit Ganguli, Surya Sripathi Raju and Molina Sharma]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Wed, 08 Jul 2026 16:00:00 +0000</pubDate>

		<guid isPermaLink="false">https://azure.microsoft.com/en-us/blog/?p=52552</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[<p>Cloud resiliency is about ensuring systems can adapt, recover, and keep functioning within real-world constraints.</p>

]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[
<aside class="table-of-contents-block accordion wp-block-bloginabox-theme-table-of-contents" id="accordion-507c5bb2-76b3-42b0-a862-223ebd7f186c" data-bi-aN="table-of-contents">

	<div id="accordion-collapse-507c5bb2-76b3-42b0-a862-223ebd7f186c" class="table-of-contents-block__collapse-wrapper collapse show" data-parent="#accordion-507c5bb2-76b3-42b0-a862-223ebd7f186c">

			<ol class="table-of-contents-block__list"><li class="table-of-contents-block__list-item"><a class="table-of-contents-block__list-item-link" href="#resiliency-as-a-shared-responsibility-not-a-handoff">Resiliency as a shared responsibility, not a handoff</a></li><li class="table-of-contents-block__list-item"><a class="table-of-contents-block__list-item-link" href="#platform-foundations-that-reflect-reality-zones-regions-and-sovereignty">Platform foundations that reflect reality: zones, regions, and sovereignty</a></li><li class="table-of-contents-block__list-item"><a class="table-of-contents-block__list-item-link" href="#azure-features-and-capabilities-strengthen-resiliency-outcomes">Azure features and capabilities strengthen resiliency outcomes</a></li><li class="table-of-contents-block__list-item"><a class="table-of-contents-block__list-item-link" href="#bridging-intent-to-execution-through-experiences-on-azure">Bridging intent to execution through experiences on Azure</a></li><li class="table-of-contents-block__list-item"><a class="table-of-contents-block__list-item-link" href="#how-you-can-build-resilience-in-azure">How you can build Resilience in Azure</a></li><li class="table-of-contents-block__list-item"><a class="table-of-contents-block__list-item-link" href="#azure-essentials">Azure Essentials</a></li></ol>		</div>

	<span class="table-of-contents-block__progress-bar"></span>
</aside>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Resiliency in the cloud is often described in terms of availability, such as how quickly a system fails over, how many replicas exist, or what a service-level agreement guarantees. But for most organizations today, especially those operating in regulated, sovereign, or geopolitically sensitive environments, resiliency is something far more fundamental. It is the ability to continue operating under pressure, protect what matters most, and recover safely when the unexpected happens.</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">A useful way to think about this is not a system problem, but a city problem. A modern city does not depend on a single power source, a single road, or a single control system. It is designed to withstand disruptions, whether from infrastructure failures, natural events, or security incidents. It has redundancy—but more importantly—it has governance, control, and recovery mechanisms that reflect local realities. <a href="https://azure.microsoft.com/en-us/solutions/resiliency" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Cloud resiliency</a> operates in much the same way. It is not just about avoiding outages; it is about ensuring systems can adapt, recover, and keep functioning within real-world constraints.</p>



<div class="wp-block-buttons is-content-justification-center is-layout-flex wp-container-core-buttons-is-layout-a89b3969 wp-block-buttons-is-layout-flex">
<div class="wp-block-button"><a class="wp-block-button__link wp-element-button" href="https://azure.microsoft.com/en-us/solutions/resiliency" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Get started with Resiliency in Azure</a></div>
</div>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">On Azure, resiliency is not something Microsoft delivers <em>to</em> customers. It is something Microsoft builds <em>with</em> them. The platform provides deeply resilient infrastructure and increasingly intelligent capabilities, but resiliency outcomes only emerge when those are intentionally designed, aligned with sovereignty constraints, and continuously validated against real-world conditions. Last year, we explained how at its core, <a href="https://techcommunity.microsoft.com/blog/azuregovernanceandmanagementblog/improve-your-resiliency-posture-with-new-capabilities-and-intelligent-assistance/4469771" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Azure approaches resiliency across three interconnected pillars</a>: <strong>infrastructure resiliency, data resiliency, and cyber recovery.</strong></p>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Infrastructure resiliency</strong>: ensuring applications remain available through failure conditions.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Data resiliency</strong>: ensuring data remains protected, durable, and recoverable.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Cyber recovery</strong>: ensuring organizations can recover safely from compromised states.</li>
</ul>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Together, they ensure not only that systems remain available, but that they remain <strong>recoverable and trustworthy</strong>—even when failure modes are unpredictable. These pillars are operationalized through a lifecycle approach that helps organizations design, improve, and continuously validate their resiliency posture.</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">What differentiates Azure is how these elements come together. Azure provides not just <a href="https://azure.microsoft.com/en-us/solutions/resiliency" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">resilient infrastructure</a>, but a unified approach that spans platform capabilities, observability, validation, and intelligent remediation, allowing organizations to move from designing for resiliency to continuously operating and improving it.</p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="resiliency-as-a-shared-responsibility-not-a-handoff">Resiliency as a shared responsibility, not a handoff</h2>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">In any city, infrastructure providers ensure that roads, utilities, and foundational systems are reliable. But how buildings are designed, how emergency plans are executed, and how critical services are protected; those remain the responsibility of the city and its operators.</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Azure’s shared responsibility model follows the same principle. Microsoft is responsible for delivering a <strong>resilient cloud platform foundation like </strong>regions, physical datacenters, networking, isolation boundaries, and engineering systems that reduce blast radius and improve durability at scale. This includes capabilities such as Availability Zones, regional isolation, and services like <strong><a href="https://azure.microsoft.com/en-us/products/backup" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Azure Backup</a> and <a href="https://azure.microsoft.com/en-us/products/site-recovery" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Azure Site Recovery</a></strong>. Customers then build on Azure enabled experiences to configure the right capabilities and achieve their desired resiliency outcomes. This includes how applications are architected, how dependencies are managed, how recovery objectives are defined, and how backup and disaster recovery are configured and tested. In sovereign and regulated environments, this responsibility becomes even more critical where customers explicitly define where data resides, how it moves, and how recovery aligns with compliance and jurisdictional requirements.</p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="platform-foundations-that-reflect-reality-zones-regions-and-sovereignty">Platform foundations that reflect reality: zones, regions, and sovereignty</h2>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Modern <a href="https://azure.microsoft.com/en-us/solutions/resiliency" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Azure resiliency</a> starts with a <strong>zone-first design approach</strong>, where applications are built to tolerate the loss of an entire Availability Zone. This significantly reduces the likelihood of localized infrastructure failures impacting application availability.</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">However, resilience does not stop at zones. Regions themselves are not uniform, and assuming uniformity is one of the most common causes of design fragility.</p>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li class="wp-block-list-item">Some Azure regions are <strong>paired</strong>, with predefined recovery regions aligned for disaster recovery.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">Others are <strong>non-paired</strong>, often due to sovereignty, regulatory, or geographic constraints.</li>
</ul>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">This distinction fundamentally shapes resiliency architecture.</p>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Paired region scenario (predictable recovery): </strong>Azure provides a spectrum of durability options from locally redundant storage (LRS) to zone redundant (ZRS) and geo‑redundant storage (GRS), enabling customers to align data protection strategies with their availability, compliance, and data sovereignty requirements. For example, a financial services application deployed in West Europe can leverage its paired region (North Europe) for disaster recovery. Using Azure Site Recovery (ASR), workloads are continuously replicated and orchestrated to enable application-level continuity during a regional disruption. 
<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li class="wp-block-list-item">The predefined region pairing offers predictable failover behavior, along with well-understood Recovery Point Objective (RPO) and Recovery Time Objective (RTO) trade-offs. However, modern Azure resiliency guidance has evolved beyond strict reliance on region pairs. As outlined in the <a href="https://techcommunity.microsoft.com/blog/reliability-and-resiliency-in-azure/modern-azure-resilience-with-mark-russinovich/4508967">Modern Azure Resilience with Mark Russinovich</a> blog, customers are increasingly adopting flexible multi-region architectures, including non-paired region strategies based on factors such as service availability, capacity, latency, and data residency requirements. These patterns emphasize that disaster recovery is no longer bound to predefined pairs, but instead is a design choice aligned to workload-specific needs.</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">In such scenarios, Azure Site Recovery plays a critical role by providing consistent, application-aware replication and failover orchestration across any chosen region, paired or not. This allows customers to standardize their recovery strategy while retaining the flexibility to meet evolving business, regulatory, and scale considerations.</p>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Non-paired region scenario (sovereign constraint): </strong>a government workload operates in a sovereign region with no predefined pair. Cross-region recovery is restricted. The architecture prioritizes zonal high availability and restore-based recovery using backup to region of choice, ensuring data remains within jurisdictional boundaries. Recovery is slower but fully compliant.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Asymmetric recovery scenario (regulated enterprise): </strong>a multinational enterprise deploys in a constrained geography where only subsets of data can leave the region. For example, Azure Site Recovery enables failover for critical services, while sensitive data relies on Azure Backup for in-boundary recovery. The result is an intentionally asymmetric resiliency model, balancing compliance with business continuity.</li>
</ul>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">The result is a shift from one-size-fits-all architectures to workload-driven resiliency design, where recovery strategies are intentionally aligned to business, regulatory, and operational constraints.</p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="azure-features-and-capabilities-strengthen-resiliency-outcomes">Azure features and capabilities strengthen resiliency outcomes</h2>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph"><a href="https://azure.microsoft.com/en-us/solutions/resiliency" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Resiliency in Azure</a> is not delivered by a single service, but it is achieved through a <strong>set of capabilities and services</strong>. These capabilities work together to ensure applications remain available, data remains protected, and systems can recover even under infrastructure failures, regional disruptions, or cyber-attacks. It begins with <strong>zone-resilient foundations</strong> that reduce exposure to localized failures, and extends through <strong>autoscaling, load balancing, and health-aware traffic management</strong> that keeps applications responsive under stress. </p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">For broader infrastructure or regional disruptions, <strong>Azure Site Recovery</strong> enables continuity through replication and failover orchestration. Equally important, <strong>Azure Backup</strong> addresses a different class of risk like corruption, accidental deletion, compliance retention, and cyber compromise by enabling recovery to a trusted point in time when failover is not enough. These capabilities are most effective when paired with <strong>strong observability and rehydration-friendly design</strong>, where systems can detect issues early, recover automatically, and rebuild quickly. The result is a more complete view of resiliency: not just maintaining uptime but sustaining trust and recoverability under real-world failure conditions.</p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="bridging-intent-to-execution-through-experiences-on-azure">Bridging intent to execution through experiences on Azure</h2>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Customers had tools but lacked a unified way to measure and improve their resiliency posture. Introduced at Microsoft Build 2026 and available in public preview, <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/resiliency/" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"><strong>Azure Infrastructure Resiliency Manager</strong></a> addresses this challenge. It provides an <strong>application-centric and resource-centric view of resiliency</strong>, bringing together <a href="https://azure.microsoft.com/en-us/solutions/resiliency" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Resiliency in Azure</a>, <a href="https://azure.microsoft.com/en-us/products/advisor" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Azure Advisor</a>, <a href="https://azure.microsoft.com/en-us/products/chaos-studio" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Azure Chaos Studio</a>, and <a href="https://azure.microsoft.com/en-us/products/monitor" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Azure Monitor</a> into a single, cohesive experience. </p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">A key starting point is <strong>zonal resiliency posture</strong>. It helps customers understand whether their workloads are truly zone-resilient, identify hidden dependencies, and pinpoint gaps between intended architecture and actual deployment.</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">It introduces a lifecycle approach to resiliency:</p>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Start resilient</strong>: design workloads with the right foundational posture.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Get resilient</strong>: identify and close gaps in existing systems.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Stay resilient:</strong> continuously validate and improve through drills and monitoring.</li>
</ul>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">At the core of Azure Infrastructure Resiliency Manager is the <strong><a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/copilot/resiliency-agent" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Resiliency Agent</a></strong>, which brings intelligence and automation into the lifecycle. The agent evaluates workloads holistically and identifies risks, surfaces misconfigurations, and explains trade-offs across cost, availability, and compliance. But its role extends beyond analysis. This represents a shift from reactive guidance to proactive and increasingly autonomous resiliency management.</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">In addition to guiding remediation, the Resiliency Agent can generate <strong>Infrastructure-as-Code (IaC) templates</strong>, enabling teams to directly implement recommended changes in their deployment pipelines. This is a fundamental shift: resiliency moves from being <strong>advisory to executable</strong>. It becomes embedded in DevOps workflows; codified, repeatable, and consistently applied.</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">In addition to this, with the <strong>Azure Backup MCP Server</strong>, these capabilities become programmable. Organizations can integrate backup posture validation, recovery readiness checks, and policy-driven restore workflows into automated systems while maintaining full control within sovereignty boundaries.</p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="how-you-can-build-resilience-in-azure">How you can build Resilience in Azure</h2>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">On Azure, this evolution reflects a shift from predefined constructs to intentional architectures, from fragmented tools to unified experiences, and from guidance to execution. As organizations navigate increasing complexity, regulatory constraints, and unpredictable failure modes, the path forward is clear: build resilience into the foundation, validate it continuously, and automate it wherever possible. With Azure’s platform capabilities, application-centric experiences, and intelligent agents, resiliency is not just achievable but operationalized to deliver with confidence.</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Explore <a href="https://azure.microsoft.com/en-us/solutions/azure-essentials" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Azure Essentials</a> to get started with a unified resiliency experience across your applications and infrastructure. Azure Essentials, Microsoft Unified, and <a href="https://azure.microsoft.com/en-us/solutions/azure-accelerate" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">Azure Accelerate</a> help organizations move from resiliency design to operational execution across every stage of the lifecycle.</p>

		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Salim Chawro]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Tue, 07 Jul 2026 16:00:00 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Management and governance]]></category>

					<description><![CDATA[<p>Azure Key Vault Managed Hardware Security Module (HSM) provides strong sovereignty over your encryption keys. Keys are generated and stored in a single-tenant, FIPS 140-3 Level 3 HSM that only you control: Microsoft has no access to your key material, and you govern who can use each key.</p>
<p>The post <a href="https://azure.microsoft.com/en-us/blog/external-key-management-for-azure-managed-hsm-is-now-in-public-preview/">External key management for Azure Managed HSM is now in public preview</a> appeared first on <a href="https://azure.microsoft.com/en-us/blog">Microsoft Azure Blog</a>.</p>
]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[
<p class="wp-block-paragraph"><a href="https://aka.ms/mhsmekm" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">Azure Key Vault Managed Hardware Security Module (HSM)</a> provides strong sovereignty over your encryption keys. Keys are generated and stored in a single-tenant, FIPS 140-3 Level 3 HSM that only you control: Microsoft has no access to your key material, and you govern who can use each key. For most organizations, including those with stringent regulatory requirements, this level of control is sufficient.</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">
  Some organizations have a further requirement: the hardware that holds their key must reside physically outside Azure datacenters. <strong>External key management for Azure Key Vault Managed HSM is now in public preview</strong> to address that requirement, delivering on <a href="https://blogs.microsoft.com/blog/2025/06/16/announcing-comprehensive-sovereign-solutions-empowering-european-organizations/">a commitment made a year ago</a>.
</p>



<div class="wp-block-buttons is-content-justification-center is-layout-flex wp-container-core-buttons-is-layout-a89b3969 wp-block-buttons-is-layout-flex">
<div class="wp-block-button"><a class="wp-block-button__link wp-element-button" href="https://aka.ms/mhsmekm" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer noreferrer noopener"><strong style="font-family: inherit;font-size: 15.008px;font-style: inherit;letter-spacing: 0.30016px;text-transform: inherit">Try <strong>External key management for Azure Key Vault Managed HSM</strong></strong></a></div>
</div>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="how-managed-hsm-delivers-sovereignty-today"><a id="post-52575-X6153d177f958023adb85f2376ce55c910fb68bf"></a>How Managed HSM delivers sovereignty today</h2>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">
  Before looking at external key management, it’s worth being precise about the sovereignty <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/key-vault/managed-hsm/overview">Managed HSM</a> already provides. Managed HSM is a single-tenant service: each instance is a dedicated cluster of FIPS 140-3 Level 3 validated HSM partitions for each customer—built on Marvell LiquidSecurity adapters. Keys are generated inside that hardware and never leave it in plaintext, making the keys inaccessible to Microsoft operators.
</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Control rests with you, not Microsoft:</p>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Customer-specific security domain.</strong> Each HSM cluster is cryptographically isolated by a security domain that you generate and own. Microsoft can’t decrypt your key material or recover your HSM cluster without it. You are in full control of the security domain as it’s protection and safeguarding is outside of Microsoft.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Multiperson control.</strong> The security domain is protected by a quorum of RSA key pairs that you hold offline. Recovery requires your quorum, so no single person—and no Microsoft operator—can act alone.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Local role-based access control</strong> (<strong>RBAC).</strong> A data-plane authorization model, independent of <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/role-based-access-control/overview">Azure RBAC</a>, governs who can perform each cryptographic operation.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Key attestation.</strong> You can obtain cryptographic proof that a key was generated and is used within the FIPS 140-3 Level 3 hardware boundary.</li>
</ul>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">
  Managed HSM is built on FIPS 140-3 Level 3 HSMs and confidential computing technology based on Intel SGX, so request handling, access control, and key material are isolated in hardware enclaves and HSMs that no Microsoft operator—even one with administrative or physical access to the host—can read. Managed HSM provides redundancy, isolation, and protection—giving organizations the sovereignty assurances they need without compromising on key security, operational overhead or availability. 
</p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="what-external-key-management-adds"><a id="post-52575-what-external-key-management-adds"></a>What external key management adds</h2>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">
  Managed HSM already provides full customer control over your keys, with enterprise-grade availability, security, and operational simplicity. External key management adds one capability: the option to keep your key material on an HSM that you own and operate, either on-premises or with a trusted third party, completely outside Microsoft infrastructure.
</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">
  External key management is designed for scenarios where regulation or contractual obligations mandate the cryptographic keys must reside outside the cloud provider’s environment. These requirements are sometimes found in highly regulated sectors such as government, financial services, and critical infrastructure, and in jurisdictions with strict data-sovereignty rules. External key management ensures the root of trust and key material remain on hardware you own and operate, outside Microsoft infrastructure, and under your direct physical control. 
</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">
  However, this model should only be adopted deliberately and only when required. For most workloads, Managed HSM keys remain the recommended approach, delivering higher native availability, reduced operational complexity, and a security posture that meets or exceeds sovereignty requirements without introducing additional risk or overhead. External key management is about meeting specific regulatory constraints, not increasing baseline security. When those constraints do not apply, Managed HSM provides a stronger, more reliable, and more operationally efficient solution.
</p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="how-it-works"><a id="post-52575-how-it-works"></a>How it works</h2>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">
  External key management extends Managed HSM through a dedicated API endpoint that connects directly to the HSM you control. It allows cryptographic operations in Azure to invoke external key material without changing how applications interact with the service. The external key never resides in or passes through Microsoft infrastructure; only your hardware uses it. Because you control that hardware, you can disconnect it at any time to halt all cryptographic operations.
</p>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Integration is transparent to applications.</strong> Applications continue to use Managed HSM and the Azure Key Vault API with the customer-managed key envelope encryption pattern unchanged. When an data access requires your external key to decrypt local data encryption keys, Managed HSM forwards it to your hardware and returns the result.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>You choose the hardware and partner.</strong> Because the external key management API is an open specification, you decide how to implement it. Your hardware, your partner, or your implementation.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>All connections are mutually authenticated and encrypted.</strong> Traffic between Azure and your hardware is secured with mutual TLS, ensuring a secure and trusted connection between <a href="https://azure.microsoft.com/en-us">Azure</a> and your HSM.</li>
</ul>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="hsm-ecosystem"><a id="post-52575-hsm-ecosystem"></a>HSM ecosystem</h2>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">
  A growing ecosystem of HSM vendors support integration with the Managed HSM external key management API, as many providers are actively enabling compatibility for their platforms.
</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Microsoft doesn’t build or operate the connecting integration proxy itself. Instead, you benefit from an open model: you can use a vendor provided implementation, reply on a partner to operate it, or build your own.</p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="responsibilities-and-tradeoffs"><a id="post-52575-responsibilities-and-tradeoffs"></a>Responsibilities and tradeoffs</h2>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">
  External key management deliberately shifts a portion of operational responsibility to you. This is the direct consequence of extending the trust boundary beyond Azure: you gain control over the root of trust, and with it, ownership of the systems that enforce it.
</p>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Availability of your hardware.</strong> The Managed HSM SLA applies up to the point Managed HSM calls your external HSM proxy. Availability of your proxy and HSM is your responsibility. Any disruption on your side directly impacts cryptographic operations and Azure service data accessibility.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Scope of operations.</strong> External key management focuses on the operations used to protect data at rest. It does not expose the full set of key operations available with Managed HSM keys, reflecting a deliberate trade-off between control and functionality.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Hardware operations.</strong> Provisioning, securing, scaling, monitoring, and recovery of your proxy and HSM become your responsibility, whether operated directly or through a partner.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Error transparency.</strong> Failures originating on your side of the connection are surfaced in Managed HSM logs, but remain your responsibility to diagnose and resolve.</li>
</ul>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">
  This is the core trade-off: more control means more responsibility.
</p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="public-preview-scope"><a id="post-52575-public-preview-scope"></a>Public preview scope</h2>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Availability:</strong> all Azure public regions at preview launch.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Access:</strong> gated. Your Microsoft account team enables external key management on your Managed HSM—contact them to request it.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Use case:</strong> protecting data at rest for <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/security/fundamentals/encryption-customer-managed-keys-support">Azure services that support customer-managed keys with Managed HSM</a>.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Pricing:</strong> standard <a href="https://azure.microsoft.com/en-us/pricing/details/key-vault/" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Managed HSM pricing</a>, with no additional Microsoft surcharge. You cover the cost of your own hardware and any partner licensing.</li>
</ul>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="get-started"><a id="post-52575-get-started"></a>Get started</h2>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li class="wp-block-list-item">Start with: <a href="https://aka.ms/mhsmekmstart" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">What is Managed HSM external key management?</a></li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">Review the <a href="https://aka.ms/mhsmekmsla" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">SLA and shared-responsibility model</a></li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">Try the <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/key-vault/managed-hsm/external-key-management-quickstart-cli" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">Azure CLI quickstart</a></li>
</ul>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">
  External key management is the latest step in giving customers granular control over how and where their keys are protected. During public preview, your feedback will directly shape the feature on its path to general availability — including the operational guidance, vendor integrations, and scenarios we prioritize next.
</p>

<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="take-control-of-your-encryption-keys">Take control of your encryption keys</h2>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Explore external key management for Managed HSM and keep your key material outside Azure while maintaining secure, scalable operations.</p>
